This should work fine for detecting which language provider handles the file.\n\nLet me now think about edge cases:1. When a file is created on disk - We ignore create events in the file watcher (ignoreCreateEvents: true)\n2. When a file is modified on disk - We clear diagnostics and request re-analysis\n3. When a file is deleted on disk - We clear its diagnostics\n\nThis should be sufficient for the git branch switching scenario because:- When switching branches, files might change content (modification events)\n- Files might be deleted in the new branch (delete events)\n- Files might be created in the new branch (but we're ignoring creates, which is fine because there won't be stale diagnostics for new files)\n\nActually, wait - I need to think about this more carefully. When a file is modified on disk and no editor has it open:1. We detect the file change via file watcher\n2. We clear diagnostics via `onFileChanged()`\n3. We request `getErr()` to re-analyze the file\n\nBut `getErr()` might only work for files that are currently open in the editor. Let me check if `getErr()` can be called for files that aren't open.\n\nLooking at the code, `getErr()` is called with a list of resources and it requests diagnostics for those files from the TypeScript server. The TypeScript server should be able to analyze files that aren't open in the editor, as long as they're part of the project.\n\n\nThere's a potential issue when files are open in the editor while changing on disk. If the user reloads the file after VS Code prompts them, `onDidChangeTextDocument` fires and triggers a refresh through the normal flow. But if they don't reload, the file watcher detects the change and we clear diagnostics while requesting re-analysis, which could lead to duplicate refresh requests—though the TypeScript server should handle this gracefully by just re-analyzing and sending updated diagnostics.\n\nI should verify the error handling works correctly. The `getErr()` method should safely queue requests even if the server isn't ready, so that should be fine. The file watcher setup looks solid—it creates watchers for each workspace folder with patterns matching TypeScript and JavaScript files, which should be efficient and cover the necessary files. One concern is whether `language.handlesUri(uri)` will reliably find the right language provider for all file extensions we care about.\n\nLooking at how `handlesUri` works, it checks both standard extensions and config files, so coverage should be comprehensive. Even if a file doesn't match any language provider, calling `getErr()` on it is harmless—the TypeScript server will simply ignore non-TypeScript files.
